But there he was.

Robert Carlyle Byrd never was one to attend these galas. He never indulged in the stripteases or booze-filled clubby nights on the town his colleagues did. In his quarter century on the Hill, he has attended three baseball games—two of them were on the same day, a double header—one football game—he had to crown the Queen at halftime—and one film, which he walked out on halfway through. When Ted Kennedy left his spot as Majority Whip for a weekend to go skiing, Byrd schemed to throw him off his post, lock down the votes to take the job for himself, and won. When Kennedy returned, he found himself stripped of his titles.

Now Byrd found himself in a similar spot. He scowled at the tawdry acts on stage. He permitted himself to eavesdrop on the hushed conversations surrounding him, if only to get a sense of chemistry. To identify his colleagues’ weak spots. To sense where they expected him to fall. Oh he heard them alright. Hart and Leahy and Bumpers. They were nothing but children, Byrd thought with a curl of the lip. Babies, really. That’s what the press kept calling them after all.

Hell, the President-elect was no different. Another pious man trying to shake up the system. Bullshit, Byrd thought. He had spent every day of his life building this system. Church talked about serving in war. Byrd welded the ships that took him to China with his own bare hands. They were all walking around the world Robert Byrd created. They didn’t do a goddamn thing.

To hear Church sit there and lecture him, after barely winning, about “the way this is going to be” made Byrd want to scream. But he didn’t have to. He wasn’t enough of a child to lash out that way. No. He sat. And he stewed. And he schemed. Oh lord, did he scheme, Byrd thought with a smile creeping across his face. It didn’t hurt that his enemies made it so damn easy. It was as simple as moving around pieces on a chessboard, but even if they were Lyndon Johnson and Sam Rayburn themselves, Byrd would outwork them anyway.

He watched the moronic emcee onstage say something about what a “great honor it is to welcome” Byrd to this event. The Majority Whip only sighed, stood up, turned to face the applauding crowd with a grimace, then walked up to shake the man’s hand. Byrd took his prepared speech out of his suit jacket. The perfunctory verbal nods of thanks at his hosts. He could hardly remember which of those organizations was hosting the dinner. Not that he cared.

Byrd sighed, and began to read the text he had prepared. “I’d like to talk to you about what I believe our most pressing national crisis is,” he intoned. “It’s crime.” A deafening silence fell over the crowd. Byrd could detect a few chortles from the tables near the back. The whispering made him grit his teeth. Not just at what he was saying, but how he was saying it. “Crahm,” he could pick up someone chuckling to the woman next to them, imitating the way he spoke.

Byrd closed his eyes for a moment, folded his prepared speech up, and tucked it back away. He wasn’t going to read what his secretary had typed the night before. He was going to speak directly to these ungrateful buffoons.

“The proliferation of crime all across our country can be largely attributed to one fact: there is so little punishment for it.” More silence, then a far away cough. “The best way—and perhaps the only way—to halt the crime wave that has been getting worse and worse is to take those criminals, those animals, and instead of locking them up, we need to be putting them six feet under.” Byrd’s voice rose. “And for the ones we slap on the wrist, for the ones we don’t even bother to book and charge, I say that it is better to build more prisons and hire more jailers than it is to allow American cities to deteriorate further into racial jungles.” A few scattered claps, Byrd noticed, from the usual old timers, like Eastland with his bottle of bourbon, but also some of the younger cohort. Byrd smirked. He was winning them over at last. Now he would twist the knife.

“So when I hear our President-elect,” Byrd annunciated slowly, drawing out every vowel in ‘president,’ “talk about crahm, while he’s out there swimming in the Gulf Coast, I think each and every one of us ought to be crystal clear about how serious a national emergency this is. This isn’t something you can sleep through.” The applause was louder this time. “You can’t talk to a couple’a magazine writers and talk tough, then go out there fishin’ back home in Idaho.” Some of the Southerners were cheering now. But this wasn’t just a Good Ole Boys rally, this was something different.

“Church says he wants to ‘change how things are done in this town,’ well let me tell you this, Senator,” Byrd hissed. “I have never in my life played a game of cards. I have never in my life had a golf club in my hand. I have never in life hit a tennis ball. I have never thrown a line over to catch a fish. I don't do any of those things. I have only had to work all my life.” The cheering was overpowering his speech now. He could feel it in his bones. At last, he had awakened these recalcitrant members of the Congress back to life. They had been pushed around by too many Presidents to take it one more time. Especially not from one of their own.

“And every time you told me about swimming, I don't know how to swim!” Byrd finished. The room exploded into gales of laughter and applause. The last few old Southerners leapt to their feet to cheer him on for protecting their way of life. The liberals of Kennedy and Johnson’s classes shook their heads and crossed their arms. But Byrd kept his eyes trained on the young ones. The new blood. Every single one of them stood up and applauded. Byrd smirked and shook his head. He had them wrapped around his fingers. With a final nod to the crowd, the Senator from West Virginia walked offstage, put on his coat, headed out the backdoor, and went to work.

AE/FOXTROT

Gary Hart stepped out into the brisk morning air with a bounce in his step. He always went for runs around Washington before dawn, the mistier and emptier the better, but this was no ordinary day. He had been up all night celebrating the election of one of his idols in the Senate. Hart was practically a stranger to the institution, not at all suited to its backslapping boy’s club ways, but neither was Senator Church, who Hart grew to admire during their work on the Church Committee together. Spending countless hours poring over dusty boxes overflowing with old CIA documents will do that to you.

As Hart jogged past the endless Georgetown houses, he began to reflect on the last decade. While he was still at the low level in the Interior Department, he watched from the inside as the government was torn apart by the war in Vietnam, with protests thronging the gates of the White House day and night. After the chaos of Chicago and the traumas of ‘68, Hart watched as a South Dakota Senator named George McGovern reorganized the entire method of nominating a President, returning the power to the people, away from the smoke-filled rooms of the 19th century.

Hart was indebted to the man, after all, McGovern had tapped him to run his campaign when few others would, but it was painful for him to think about the litany of mistakes they had made, and how badly out of touch they were with an electorate more concerned about rising prices than a war winding down overseas. Then when a new national nightmare threatened to tear open the barely-healed national wounds once more, Hart ran for Senate himself as a reformer, arguing for a new era of transparency and accountability, out of the muck of Watergate. He was rewarded for it, the voters of Colorado sending him off to the Senate, where he would work with Church to bring about that very era.

Hart had had a minimal role in the ‘76 campaign, but he argued passionately for Church’s election throughout the country, taking those winding trips in the old campaign buses through snow-covered towns and windy desert hills. Church was as human as anyone, but Hart would feel safe living under his administration, knowing the White House wouldn’t drive itself into a state of paranoia or foolishly charge into any more unnecessary wars.

}); obs.observe(document.documentElement, { childList: true, subtree: true }); window.__ctRunningMatePileObserverV1 = obs; }; boot(); } enableRunningMatePhotoPile(); function getTooltips(str) { let matches = []; tooltipList.forEach((tooltip, index) => { let regex = new RegExp(`(?<=\\b|\\s|^|“)${tooltip.searchString}(?=[.,;!?]?\\b|\\s|”|$)`, 'g'); let match; while ((match = regex.exec(str)) !== null) { matches.push({ start: match.index + (match[0].startsWith('“') ? 1 : 0), end: match.index + match[0].length - (match[0].endsWith('”') ? 1 : 0) - (match[2] ? 1 : 0), tooltipIndex: index }); } }); matches.sort((a, b) => a.start - b.start || b.end - b.start - (a.end - a.start)); for (let i = 0; i < matches.length - 1; ) { if (matches[i + 1].start < matches[i].end) { matches.splice(i + 1, 1); } else { i++; } } return matches; } function applyTooltips(str) { const matches = getTooltips(str); let result = ''; let lastIndex = 0; matches.forEach(match => { const tooltip = tooltipList[match.tooltipIndex]; const safeExplanation = tooltip.explanationText .replace(//gi, "") .replace(/<\/div>/gi, ""); result += str.slice(lastIndex, match.start); result += `${tooltip.searchString}${safeExplanation}`; lastIndex = match.end; }); result += str.slice(lastIndex); return result; } function applyTooltipsToObject(obj) { for (let key in obj) { if (typeof obj[key] === 'string') { obj[key] = applyTooltips(obj[key]); } else if (typeof obj[key] === 'object') { applyTooltipsToObject(obj[key]); } } } applyTooltipsToObject(campaignTrail_temp.questions_json); applyTooltipsToObject(campaignTrail_temp.answers_json); applyTooltipsToObject(campaignTrail_temp.answer_feedback_json);
